        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="200e35d947344ed2b62e60a520bb40c5"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="200e35d947344ed2b62e60a520bb40c5">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '743f20a5-8768-4611-95c5-58d8ff7c916b'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = 'AD8A4C87E08D77351F9767603B0E457A';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d61814889" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted tremendous interest and become a worldwide research hotpot owing to their low cost and abundant resources. To obtain suitable anode materials with excellent performance for SIBs, an effective and controllable strategy is presented to fabricate SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets coating on nanohollow cubic CoS<sub>2</sub>/C (CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>) composites with a hollow structure using Co-metal-organic frameworks as the starting material. As anodes for SIBs, the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> electrode exhibits ultralong cycle life and excellent rate performance, which can maintain a high specific capacity of 400.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even after 3500 cycles at a current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. When used in a full-cell, it also shows enhanced sodium storage properties and delivers a high reversible capacity of 567.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. This strategy can pave a way for preparing various metal sulfides with fascinating structure and excellent performance for the potential application in energy storage area.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802716-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802716-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the development of society, the following problems such as environmental pollution and short of resources call for sustainable sources of energy and innovative technologies for both energy generation and storage. As one of the current major energy storage technologies, rechargeable lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have achieved commercial success and been widely applied in portable devices, electronic vehicles and renewable energy storage devices.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0001_R_d61814881e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0005_R_d61814881e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">5</a></span> Unfortunately, the high cost and limited lithium resources have driven up their prices and hindered their expanded application in large-scale energy devices. Owing to their low cost and abundant resources, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are undoubtedly the most promising alternative energy storage technology to LIBs.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0006_R_d61814881e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Science">6</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0008_R_d61814881e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a></span></p>
         
         <p>To date, SIBs have attracted tremendous interest and become worldwide research hotpot owing to the high relative abundance of Na element. However, SIBs still face many technical problems compared to LIBs, especially the limited choice of anode materials. Although a number of studies have been performed to seek for effective anode materials for SIBs, they still suffer from low specific capacity, bad rate capability, and short cycle life.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0009_R_d61814881e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0012_R_d61814881e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></span> In particular, the radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (0.102 nm) is much larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.069 nm), which not only hinders its movement but also leads to severe volume expansion of the host materials.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0013_R_d61814881e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0016_R_d61814881e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Sci.">16</a></span> Therefore, it is still a great challenge to develop appropriate anode materials with stable microstructure, high reversible and long cycling life for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In order to obtain suitable electrode materials for SIBs, many efforts have been devoted to explore diverse type of anode compounds, such as hard carbons,<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0017_R_d61814881e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0018_R_d61814881e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a></span> metal and alloys,<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0019_R_d61814881e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Environ. Sci.">19</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0020_R_d61814881e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Commun.">20</a></span> metal oxide,<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0021_R_d61814881e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Lett.">21</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0022_R_d61814881e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - 2D Mater.">22</a></span> metal sulfide and phosphorus.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0023_R_d61814881e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small">23</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0025_R_d61814881e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a></span> Among these materials, metal sulfides, especially with layered structure, have exhibited great electrochemical performance due to their rich redox reactions, good mechanical and thermal stability.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0026_R_d61814881e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0028_R_d61814881e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nat. Mater.">28</a></span> However, some major challenges still need to be solved, such as huge volume variation during cycling and inferior intrinsic conductivity. To overcome the above mentioned deficiencies of metal sulfide, nanoengineering and carbon hybridization have been proved to be the most effective approaches, which can not only buffer the volume change but also improve the overall conductivity. As a novel type of porous crystalline materials, metal-organic frameworks (MOFs) have drawn remarkable interests due to their large surface areas, high porosities and controllable structure. MOFs are ideal precursors for fabricating many nanostructured porous composites, such as carbons, metal oxide/carbon and metal sulfides/carbon composites, which have been widely applied in the energy storage area.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0029_R_d61814881e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0032_R_d61814881e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></span></p>
         
         <p>In this present work, we report a facial method to obtain CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> composite using Co–Co Prussian blue analogous as starting material. This method combines the hollow structure derived from anion-exchange reaction coated with ultrathin SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. After calcination, the obtained composite exhibits outstanding performance as anode material for half/full SIBs. The features of our structural design have the following three advantages: 1) The hollow morphology can serve as buffer zoom to relieve the stress during cycling. 2) The SnS<sub>2</sub> ultrathin nanosheets can shorten the diffusion distance of Na<sup>+</sup>. 3) The carbon layer derived from MOFs can strengthen the conductivity of the composite and work as fast electronic pathway. This effective method opens up a possible way to fabricate other metal sulfides with novel structure and remarkable electrochemical performance (i.e., high reversible capacity, ultralong cycling stability and excellent rate capability).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802716-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802716-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The formation mechanism of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> hierarchical core–shell nanocubes with hollow structure is schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802716-fig-0001">1</a>. First, the uniform Co-MOF nanocubes precursor is derived from a facile coprecipitation method. Then Na<sub>2</sub>S as anion exchange reagent is used to etch Co-MOF into Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> with a hollow structure. Finally, the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> composites were obtained using Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> as precursors in the present of thioacetamide (TAA) as sulfur source and SnCl<sub>4</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O as tin source, respectively. This smart morphology is beneficial for the preeminent electrochemical performance, such as ultralong cycling stability and excellent rate capability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802716-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/466e533e-d76c-4478-abf2-d056854b873f/smll201802716-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/466e533e-d76c-4478-abf2-d056854b873f/smll201802716-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/95a6f558-6126-4055-b8a6-11bf53482af5/smll201802716-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/466e533e-d76c-4478-abf2-d056854b873f/smll201802716-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802716-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802716" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the preparation of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) images of Co-MOF, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> are shown in <b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802716-fig-0002">2</a>a–f. It can be seen clearly that Co-MOF exhibits cubic morphology, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> shows a hollow cube structure and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> presents a core–shell structure with hollow CoS<sub>2</sub>/C as core and SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets as shell. The FESEM images of SnS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) are shown in Figures S1e and S2a in the Supporting Information. The crystal structure of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, SnS<sub>2</sub>, NVP, and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> were determined by X-ray diffraction (XRD). As shown in Figure S3a–c in the Supporting Information, all the diffraction peaks match well with the standard patterns of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (powder diffraction file (PDF), PDF No. 65-1765), SnS<sub>2</sub> (PDF No. 01-1010), and NVP (PDF No. 62-0345), respectively. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0004">4</a>a, all diffraction peaks are indexed to the PDF No. 03-0772 (CoS<sub>2</sub>) and the PDF No. 01-1010 (SnS<sub>2</sub>). The peaks at 32.5°, 36.5°, 40.0°, 46.5°, and 55.3° are assigned to the (200), (210), (211), (220), and (311) planes of CoS<sub>2</sub>, and the residual peaks at 15.0°, 28.3°, 32.2°, 42.2°, 50.1°, 52.6°, 58.8°, and 60.9° are indexed to the (001), (100), (101), (102), (110), (111), (200), and (201) of SnS<sub>2</sub>, respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802716-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/42fb832e-a9e0-418c-af64-5a0fe28ddc7b/smll201802716-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/42fb832e-a9e0-418c-af64-5a0fe28ddc7b/smll201802716-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9febefb9-da31-41df-8c32-08aaade1cbc7/smll201802716-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/42fb832e-a9e0-418c-af64-5a0fe28ddc7b/smll201802716-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802716-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802716" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">FESEM images of a,b) the as-prepared Co-MOF; c,d) Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>; e,f) CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>, and g–j) EDS mapping (Co, Sn, and S elements) of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images further confirm the structure of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, SnS<sub>2</sub>, NVP, and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>. The Figure S4a–i demonstrates the TEM, high-resolution TEM (HRTEM), and selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, SnS<sub>2</sub>, and NVP, respectively. As shown in <b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802716-fig-0003">3</a>a, the hollow CoS<sub>2</sub> cubes are uniformly coated by a layer of SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. From the Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0003">3</a>d, the marked interatomic distance of 0.275 and 0.246 nm correspond to the planes (200) and (210) of the inner material CoS<sub>2</sub>. The clear lattice fingers with an interspacing of 0.278 and 0.315 nm are correspond to the (101) and (100) planes of SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, respectively. The HRTEM results are consistent with XRD analysis in <b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802716-fig-0004">4</a>a, which further confirms the formation of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>. In addition, as shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0002">2</a>g–j, the elemental mapping is performed to further confirm the composition of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>, which shows that the Co (red), Sn (green), and S (yellow) elements are evenly distributed and match well with the shape of the composite. The elemental mapping of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, SnS<sub>2</sub>, and NVP are also shown in Figures S1a–d,f–h and S2b–f in the Supporting Information, respectively. The carbon element of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> comes from Co-MOF.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802716-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d2c163f4-e580-471b-aec6-524bcb31ff19/smll201802716-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d2c163f4-e580-471b-aec6-524bcb31ff19/smll201802716-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0fe8b09d-84ca-42fd-adc0-75115b60d845/smll201802716-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d2c163f4-e580-471b-aec6-524bcb31ff19/smll201802716-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802716-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802716" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM images of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>: a,c) TEM; b) selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern; and d) high-resolution TEM (HRTEM).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802716-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bc016979-86cd-41bd-8bdb-a477c257d1b3/smll201802716-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/bc016979-86cd-41bd-8bdb-a477c257d1b3/smll201802716-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2a101dce-dda2-4b1a-9336-9d4e5fb7dfa5/smll201802716-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bc016979-86cd-41bd-8bdb-a477c257d1b3/smll201802716-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802716-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802716" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD pattern and b) XPS survey spectra of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>, c) high-resolution Co 2p, d) Sn 3d, e) S 2p, and f) C 1s XPS spectra of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopies (XPS) were conducted to investigate the elemental composition and surface chemical state of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (Figure S5a–c, Supporting Information), SnS<sub>2</sub> (Figure S5d–f, Supporting Information), and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0004">4</a>b–f). As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0004">4</a>b, the XPS survey spectrum indicates that the composite consists of Co, Sn, S, C, and O. The existence of oxide should be derived from the Co-MOF with the formation of C<span class="icomoon"></span>O and O<span class="icomoon"></span>CO (Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0004">4</a>f), or the unavoidable surface oxidation in the air.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0033_R_d61814881e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">33</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0035_R_d61814881e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Power Sources">35</a></span> The high-resolution XPS spectrum of Co 2p (Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0004">4</a>c) indicates that the Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> binding energies range from 776.9 to 790.1 eV and from 791.8 to 809.5 eV, respectively. The peaks at 778.3 and 793.4 eV are indexed to Co<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond. And the small amount of the Co<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond may be due to the partial oxidation of the surface in air. And the peaks at 780.8 and 797.2 eV are belonging to Co 2P<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2P<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, suggests that the existence of Co<span class="icomoon"></span>S bond from CoS<sub>2</sub>. The area and relative intensity suggest the majority of Co<span class="icomoon"></span>S bond.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0036_R_d61814881e815" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Mater. Lett.">36</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0037_R_d61814881e815" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Ceram. Int.">37</a></span> Meanwhile, the other two peaks located at 785.3 and 802.9 eV are attributed to the shake-up satellites.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0023_R_d61814881e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small">23</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0038_R_d61814881e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Mater.">38</a></span> The Sn 3d spectrum (Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0004">4</a>d) shows two strong peaks with binding energy values at 486.6 and 495.1 eV, corresponding to the Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sn 3d<sub>3/2</sub> photoelectron emissions, respectively, which agrees well with the Sn<sup>4+</sup> of SnS<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0039_R_d61814881e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">39</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0040_R_d61814881e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energy Environ. Sci.">40</a></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0004">4</a>e, there are two chemical states of the S species bonding with Co and Sn ions, respectively. The peaks at 161.3 and 164.2 eV shows that S<sup>2−</sup> exists in the composite, corresponding to the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of the Co<span class="icomoon"></span>S bonds, the other peaks at 161.7 and 162.8 eV are attributed to the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of the Sn<span class="icomoon"></span>S bonds, respectively.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0041_R_d61814881e846" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">41</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0043_R_d61814881e846" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Mol. Catal. A: Chem.">43</a></span> The satellite peak at 168.9 eV may be caused by the partly oxidized sulfur species on the sample surface.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0036_R_d61814881e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Mater. Lett.">36</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0044_R_d61814881e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nano Energy">44</a></span> The C 1s high-resolution XPS spectrum is given in Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0004">4</a>f, the peak at 284.7 eV is corresponding to sp<sup>2</sup>-hydridized C<span class="icomoon"></span>C or CC, the other two peaks at 285.9 and 288.5 eV are attributed to C<span class="icomoon"></span>O and O<span class="icomoon"></span>CO.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0040_R_d61814881e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energy Environ. Sci.">40</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0045_R_d61814881e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nanoscale">45</a></span></p>
         
         <p>In order to further study the specific surface area and pore size distribution of the samples, the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms were conducted at 77 K. As shown in Figure S6 in the Supporting Information, the isotherm types of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> and SnS<sub>2</sub> match well with the H-3 loop according to the modern classification of hysteresis loops. Additionally, the sample of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (Figure S6c, Supporting Information) shows a classical adsorption isotherm of type ǁ. Based on the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method, the specific surface areas are calculated to be 55.5, 24.8, and 12.8 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>, and SnS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. The pore size distribution of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> is centered at 0.94 nm (Figure S6b, Supporting Information), which can provide sufficient path for the diffusion of ions and greatly improve the electrochemical performance of the composite.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0046_R_d61814881e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Colloids Surf., A">46</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0047_R_d61814881e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">47</a></span></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The electrochemical properties of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> as anode material for SIBs were studied by cycle voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic discharge/charge cycling in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. The typical CV curves of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> is shown in <b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802716-fig-0005">5</a>a. For SnS<sub>2</sub>, a small peak at around 1.8 V in the first discharge curve is attributed to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the (001) plane.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0048_R_d61814881e914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">48</a></span> The broad reduction peak ranging from 0.3 to 0.9 V could be attributed to the conversion and alloying reactions. The discharge mechanism involves initial generation of amorphous Na<sub>2</sub>S and metallic Sn nanoparticles which further undergo the alloying reaction to form Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>. Upon charging, the dealloying reaction of Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> occurs to form metallic Sn, which further reacts with Na<sub>2</sub>S. The broad peak from 0.9 to 1.5 V is attributed to the desodiation of the Na–Sn alloy phases and the reformation of SnS<sub>2</sub>. The related reactions can be described as follows<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0049_R_d61814881e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Chem. Mater.">49</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0050_R_d61814881e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">50</a></span><div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802716-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/96931363-ac18-4453-9726-5cdc1135fd6d/smll201802716-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802716-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/545642ed-7740-4f5f-b0ba-a039afb6681e/smll201802716-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802716-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/e3a90b05-14c3-4f6a-89c2-bca1b3fe27bf/smll201802716-math-0003.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0003" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0003" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802716-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b0619dd2-0560-4637-bf6b-ab8ac2cc80dd/smll201802716-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b0619dd2-0560-4637-bf6b-ab8ac2cc80dd/smll201802716-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/579439fa-8d9f-4810-81d6-e15d7b158ba0/smll201802716-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b0619dd2-0560-4637-bf6b-ab8ac2cc80dd/smll201802716-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802716-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802716" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>: a) cyclic voltammetry curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; b) charge/discharge curves cycling in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>; c) rate performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, and SnS<sub>2</sub>; d) long-term cyclic performance at current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>; and e) Nyquist plots of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> electrodes before the cycling tests and after 50 cycles at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">For CoS<sub>2</sub>, in the first cathodic curve, two peaks at 0.57 and 0.72 V were observed, which corresponds to the formation of Co, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CoS<sub>2</sub> at the initial insertion reaction. In the first anodic stage, the other two peaks at 1.80 and 1.95 V were ascribed to the reformation of CoS<sub>2</sub>. The related reactions can be described as follows<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0041_R_d61814881e998" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">41</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0051_R_d61814881e998" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">51</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0053_R_d61814881e998" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - RSC Adv.">53</a></span><div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802716-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/c9172299-6000-415e-a33e-cad348a73ed1/smll201802716-math-0004.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0004" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0004" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802716-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/a49ce4c8-428c-46c9-be7d-cb4a75a5157e/smll201802716-math-0005.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0005" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0005" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802716-disp-0006"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/44c32861-91b9-42fe-808d-2dd39e606a30/smll201802716-math-0006.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0006" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0006" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(6)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802716-disp-0007"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/23cbb7e4-81ef-406c-8d1a-462aee32e24b/smll201802716-math-0007.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0007" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802716:smll201802716-math-0007" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(7)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>After the first cycle, there are two broad peaks in the discharge and charge at 0.3–0.9 and 0.9–1.5 V, respectively. The difference of the positions and intensities of reduction/oxidation peaks between the first and next two cycles are ascribed to the formation of solid electrolyte interphase on the surface of electrode. The discharge/charge curves show two obvious long plateaus (Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0005">5</a>b) around at 0.3–0.9 and 0.9–1.5 V which is in good line with the CV result. The second and third CV curves nearly overlap with each other, which indicate good electrochemical reactions during repeated Na<sup>+</sup> insertion–extraction.</p>
         
         <p>Good rate performance is the key factor to measure the electrochemical properties of materials. Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0005">5</a>c shows the rate performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, and SnS<sub>2</sub> at various current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 0.2, and 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Obviously, the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> electrode is obviously better than the others. The cycling performance of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, and SnS<sub>2</sub> are shown in Figure S7 in the Supporting Information, which clearly exhibits that the reversible capacity of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> can maintain 854.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 70 cycles at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Although SnS<sub>2</sub> has a high capacity at the beginning, its capacity decays rapidly during cycling. The Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> has a relatively stable cycle performance, but the capacity is much lower compared with CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>. As shown in Figure S8 in the Supporting Information, the morphology of SnS<sub>2</sub> has been destroyed after cycling, while the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> composite can maintain its initial structure, which is beneficial for excellent cycling performance. Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0005">5</a>d shows a long-term cyclic performance at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> (11.8 C, the first three cycles at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), which can retain a high reversible capacity of 400.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even after 3500 cycles with a high coulombic efficiency (CE) of 99.95% (Table S1, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To confirm the ratio of CoS<sub>2</sub> to SnS<sub>2</sub> in the sample, the inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectroscopy (ICP-AES) analysis was conducted by dissolving the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> with diluted HCl. After calculation, the ratios of carbon, CoS<sub>2</sub>, and SnS<sub>2</sub> in the sample are 13.36%, 27.17%, and 59.47%, respectively.</p>
         
         <p>From Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0005">5</a>c and Figure S7a in the Supporting Information, we can see that SnS<sub>2</sub> has high initial discharge/charge capacities. The ICP-AES result shows that SnS<sub>2</sub> accounts for larger proportion in the sample. Combining the above two points, SnS<sub>2</sub> acts as the major active material for Na storage.</p>
         
         <p>Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was also used to demonstrate the electrochemical behavior of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> as SIBs. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0005">5</a>e, the high frequency semicircle is caused by the charge transfer process, and the low frequency linearity is caused by the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion process.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0054_R_d61814881e1121" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Mater.">54</a>, <a href="#smll201802716-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0055_R_d61814881e1121" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Nat. Commun.">55</a></span> Obviously, the resistance is relatively small both before and after 50 cycles, which is beneficial for the charge transfer between electrodes and electrolyte. In addition, after 50 cycles, the charge transfer resistance becomes smaller compared with before cycling, indicating that the conductivity and electrochemical activity of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> electrode has been enhanced during cycling process.</p>
         
         <p>To further study the remarkable electrochemical performance of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> composite, CV measurements were conducted at different scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, which is a powerful way to investigate the reaction kinetic of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>. The current (<i>i</i>) measured at a specific potential (<i>V</i>) is considered to consist of two mechanisms, the capacitive contribution (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and diffusion-controlled contribution (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>), as given in the equation: <i>i</i>(<i>V</i>)  =  <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>  + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, here “<i>i</i>(<i>V</i>)” is the measured current as a function of potential “(<i>V</i>)”, and the “<i>v</i>” is scan rate, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are parameters. The relationship between the measured current (<i>i</i>) and the scan rate (<i>v</i>) based on the power law can be expressed as equation: <i>i</i>  =  <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>. The value of <i>b</i> can be obtained from the slope of the ln (<i>v</i>) − ln(<i>i</i>) curve, with a value from 0.5 to 1. If the <i>b</i> value is near 0.5, the electrochemical reaction is completely controlled by ion diffusion, when the <i>b</i> value is close to 1, pseudocapacitive behaviors are dominant. The calculated <i>b</i> values are 0.97, 0.88, 0.95, 0.98, and 0.98 (<b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802716-fig-0006">6</a>b). Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0006">6</a>c shows pseudocapacitive contributions at different scan rate, the contribution percents are 88.03%, 90.88%, 92.03%, 92.76%, and 93.42% at the scan rates of 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The capacitive behavior has high contribution at low scan, such as 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Such high capacitive may result from the following reasons: First, the hollow core–shell and nanosheet structure can provide highly accessible surface areas, which can accommodate more Na<sup>+</sup> and enhance the surface redox pseudocapacitance. Second, the discharge products (Co, Sn, Na<sub>2</sub>S) also generate large interfacial areas between them. In these interfacial areas, the CoS<sub>2</sub> and SnS<sub>2</sub> play the role of Na<sup>+</sup>-accepting phase, while the Co and Sn function as electron-accepting phase to improve the interfacial double-layer capacitance. Based on the analysis, such high capacitive benefits form the two storage model derived from the smart architecture. Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0006">6</a>d shows the pseudocapacitive fraction at the scan rate of 0.8 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The analysis shows that the pseudocapacitance Na storage accounts for a high percentage of the total capacity, which is consistent with the <i>b</i> value. The result is analogy to the behavior of metal oxides and metal chalcogenides.<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0056_R_d61814881e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Energy Environ. Sci.">56</a>-<a href="#smll201802716-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0058_R_d61814881e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - J. Phys. Chem. C﻿﻿">58</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802716-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f8fa1a15-02ab-46c9-8308-002c8d5905b9/smll201802716-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f8fa1a15-02ab-46c9-8308-002c8d5905b9/smll201802716-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3cec1f16-bb32-4391-a5c7-4d62395a8e55/smll201802716-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f8fa1a15-02ab-46c9-8308-002c8d5905b9/smll201802716-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802716-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802716" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Kinetics investigation of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>: a) CV curves at different scan rates; b) corresponding ln (peak current) versus ln (scan rate) plots at different redox states; c) bar chart showing the percent of pseudocapacitive contribution at different scan rates; d) CV curve of the pseudocapacitive shown by the gray region at a scan rate of 0.8 mV s<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Due to the excellent electrochemical performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> as Na<sup>+</sup> half-cell, the exploration of the full-cell has also been investigated. NVP is used as cathode material for the full cell and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> as anode material owing to its excellent working voltage and slight polarization in the process of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation. To ensure the maximum utilization and reasonably evaluated the electrochemical performance of the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>, the full-cells were assembled based on the capacity ratio of 1.2:1 between the NVP cathode and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> anode. The schematic diagram of full cell assembly is shown in Figure S9a in the Supporting Information.</p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802716-fig-0007">7</a>a shows the charge/discharge curves of the as-prepared full battery for the first three cycles in the voltage window of 0.01–3.0 V at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0007">7</a>c, the initial charge and discharge capacities are 780.1 and 675.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> based on the anode material, and the specific capacities can still maintain 659.0 and 659.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even after 100 cycles with a CE about 100% at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The full-cell also exhibits an excellent cycling stability, which delivers a reversible capacity of 567.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a CE of 99.91% at a high current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0007">7</a>d) after 1000 cycles. In order to verify its practical application, the as-prepared full cells are further applied to light up red light-emitting diodes (LED). As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802716-fig-0007">7</a>b, one full-cell can light up 41 LED bulbs in the shape of Shenzhen University and keep this light intensity for a period of time (Video S1, Supporting Information). The excellent electrochemical performance of both CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> as half-cell and CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>-NVP as full-cell indicates that the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> is an extremely promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201802716-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4fc4fe13-68f2-49f0-bc6d-fa024134cbf6/smll201802716-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4fc4fe13-68f2-49f0-bc6d-fa024134cbf6/smll201802716-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/359a081c-2829-4263-8100-3318333284cc/smll201802716-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4fc4fe13-68f2-49f0-bc6d-fa024134cbf6/smll201802716-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802716-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802716" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical performance of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>-NVP full-cells: a) charge/discharge curves in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>; b) digital photo of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>-NVP full-cells to light 41 LED bulbs; long-term cyclic performance at current density of c) 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and d) 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802716-sec-0030">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802716-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have successfully synthesized the core–shell CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> composite with hollow structure through a facile method. The reasonably designed CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> exhibits excellent electrochemical performance as anode material for SIBs. Importantly, the CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> composite can deliver a high specific capacity of 400.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a CE of 99.95% even after 3500 cycles at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> for half-cell. Besides, a full-cell has been fabricated with CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> as anode and excessive NVP as cathode, which can light up 41 LED bulbs and maintain a certain light intensity for a period of time. Excellent performance shows that CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> can be used as an ideal anode material for SIBs. Furthermore, this method can pave a way for preparing various metal sulfides with fascinating structure and excellent performance for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802716-sec-0040">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802716-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of Co-MOF Nanocubes</i>: Typically, 0.4 mmol K<sub>3</sub>[Co(CN)Co<sub>6</sub>] was dissolved in 20 mL deionized water (DW) followed by stirring to form a homogeneous solution A. 0.6 mmol Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.9 mmol Na<sub>3</sub>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O were dissolved in 20 mL DW to form a homogeneous solution B. Then, the obtained solution A was dropped into solution B under stirring for 10 min and aged at room temperature for 24 h. The resulting precipitates (Co-MOF) were collected by centrifugation, washed with DW and ethanol three times, and subsequently dried in a vacuum oven at 70 °C for further use.</p>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> Hollow Nanocubes</i>: Simply, 400 mg of the as-prepared Co-MOF nanocubes were dispersed in 96 mL ethanol with the assistance of ultrasonication for 30 min to form a homogeneous suspension. Then 48 mL of ethanol with 400 mg Na<sub>2</sub>S was added into the above suspension and stirred for 10 min. The solution was transferred into a Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave and heated at 120 °C for 6 h. The obtained black product Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> was centrifuged and washed with DW and ethanol three times. Finally, the product was sintered at 400 °C for 1 h under argon gas (Ar) atmosphere to increase the degree of crystallinity.</p>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> Core–Shell Nanocubes with Hollow Structure</i>: 20 mg of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> hollow nanocubes was dispersed in 20 mL ethanol and 56.3 mg of TAA was added into the above suspension. Then 105.8 mg SnCl<sub>4</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O was dissolved in 15 mL ethanol and added into the above solution under stirring for 10 min. The mixed solution was transferred into a Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave and kept at 160 °C for 12 h. After cooling to room temperature, the product CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> was harvested by centrifugation, washed sequentially with DW and ethanol. Bare SnS<sub>2</sub> was prepared through the same process without adding Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> hollow nanocubes. The product CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> and SnS<sub>2</sub> were calcined at 400 °C under Ar atmosphere to obtain a high crystallinity.</p>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of NVP Nanoparticles</i>: The typical procedure for preparation of NVP is according to previous report:<span><a href="#smll201802716-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802716-bib-0059_R_d61814881e1480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">59</a></span> 756.4 mg of H<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and 234.0 mg of NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> dissolved in 80 mL water under vigorous stirring at 80 °C for 1 h. Then 45 mg of graphene oxide (GO) was added into the solution under ultrasonication for 30 min. After that, 345.1 g of NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> and 159.0 g of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> were added into the suspension and kept stirring until dried at 80 °C. The black solid was collected and ground, then precalcined at 350 °C for 3 h under Ar atmosphere. Finally, the black produce was calcined at 700 °C in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (9/1, v/v) mixture for 10 h.</p>
         
         <p><i>Materials Characterization</i>: The morphology and structure of the samples were characterized by FESEM (JSM-7800F) and TEM (JEM2100). The composition and distribution of elements were determined by energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). XRD patterns were investigated by MiniFlex600 using nickel-filtered Cu-<i>K</i><sub>α</sub> radiation at a voltage of 40 kV and a current of 15 mA. XPS was conducted by a K-Alpha+ (Thermo Scientific) electron spectrometer. The BET (BELSORP-max) surface area was investigated to characterize the surface area and porous texture by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms at 77 K. ICP-AES (OPTIMA2100DV) was used to collect the elements content of Sn, Co.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: The electrochemical performances of half-cells and full-cells were evaluated by assembling CR2032 and CR 2025 coin cells in a high-purify argon-filled glovebox. The synthetic active materials (CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub>, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, and SnS<sub>2</sub>) were mixed with acetylene black and binder (sodium alginate) in the weight ratio of 7:2:1 to form uniform slurry in water. The slurry was uniformly pasted on copper foil and used as working electrode. The mass loading of anodes was controlled at around 1.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Sodium foil was used as counter electrode, 1.25 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethyl methyl carbonate as electrolyte and Whatman glass fiber as separator. Galvanostatic charge and discharge tests were carried out on LAND CT2001A test system in the voltage range of 0.01 to 3.0 V at 25 °C. For the full-cells assembly, the NVP was used as cathode material instead of sodium metal. The NVP electrodes were prepared with the same method as the preparation of CoS<sub>2</sub>/C@SnS<sub>2</sub> except that the copper foil changed into aluminum foil. The mass loadings of cathode and anode were controlled at around 9.5 and 1.0 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively. The schematic diagram of full cell assembly is shown in Figure S9a in the Supporting Information. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) is carried out on CHI 760E electrochemical workstation and the potential range is from 0.01 to 3 V with a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The EIS was obtained using the same electrochemical workstation in the frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.1 Hz.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="smll201802716-bib-0005"><span class="bullet">5</span><span class="author">J. B. Goodenough</span>, <span class="author">K. S. Park</span>, <i>J. Am. Chem. Soc.</i> <span class="pubYear">2013</span>,  <span class="vol">135</span>,  <span class="pageFirst">1167</span>.
